        <title>Genome and Transcriptome sequence of Finger millet ( Eleusine coracana (L.) Gaertn.) provides insights into drought tolerance and nutraceutical properties | BMC Genomics | Full Text</title>

        

    <meta name="citation_abstract" content="Finger millet (Eleusine coracana (L.) Gaertn.) is an important staple food crop widely grown in Africa and South Asia. Among the millets, finger millet has high amount of calcium, methionine, tryptophan, fiber, and sulphur containing amino acids. In addition, it has C4 photosynthetic carbon assimilation mechanism, which helps to utilize water and nitrogen efficiently under hot and arid conditions without severely affecting yield. Therefore, development and utilization of genomic resources for genetic improvement of this crop is immensely useful. Experimental results from whole genome sequencing and assembling process of ML-365 finger&#160;millet cultivar yielded 1196&#160;Mb covering approximately 82% of total estimated genome size. Genome analysis showed the presence of 85,243 genes and one half of the genome is repetitive in nature. The finger&#160;millet genome was found to have higher colinearity with foxtail millet and rice as compared to other Poaceae species. Mining of simple sequence repeats (SSRs) yielded abundance of SSRs within the finger millet genome. Functional annotation and mining of transcription factors revealed finger millet genome harbors large number of drought tolerance related genes. Transcriptome analysis of low moisture stress and non-stress samples revealed the identification of several drought-induced candidate genes, which could be used in drought tolerance breeding. This genome sequencing effort will strengthen plant breeders for allele discovery, genetic mapping, and identification of candidate genes for agronomically important traits. Availability of genomic resources of finger&#160;millet will enhance the novel breeding possibilities to address potential challenges of finger millet improvement."/>

        <meta property="og:url" content="https://bmcgenomics.biomedcentral.com/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z"/>
        <meta property="og:type" content="article"/>
        <meta property="og:site_name" content="BMC Genomics"/>
        <meta property="og:title" content="Genome and Transcriptome sequence of Finger millet ( Eleusine coracana (L.) Gaertn.) provides insights into drought tolerance and nutraceutical properties"/>
        <meta property="og:description" content="Finger millet (Eleusine coracana (L.) Gaertn.) is an important staple food crop widely grown in Africa and South Asia. Among the millets, finger millet has high amount of calcium, methionine, tryptophan, fiber, and sulphur containing amino acids. In addition, it has C4 photosynthetic carbon assimilation mechanism, which helps to utilize water and nitrogen efficiently under hot and arid conditions without severely affecting yield. Therefore, development and utilization of genomic resources for genetic improvement of this crop is immensely useful. Experimental results from whole genome sequencing and assembling process of ML-365 finger&amp;nbsp;millet cultivar yielded 1196&amp;nbsp;Mb covering approximately 82% of total estimated genome size. Genome analysis showed the presence of 85,243 genes and one half of the genome is repetitive in nature. The finger&amp;nbsp;millet genome was found to have higher colinearity with foxtail millet and rice as compared to other Poaceae species. Mining of simple sequence repeats (SSRs) yielded abundance of SSRs within the finger millet genome. Functional annotation and mining of transcription factors revealed finger millet genome harbors large number of drought tolerance related genes. Transcriptome analysis of low moisture stress and non-stress samples revealed the identification of several drought-induced candidate genes, which could be used in drought tolerance breeding. This genome sequencing effort will strengthen plant breeders for allele discovery, genetic mapping, and identification of candidate genes for agronomically important traits. Availability of genomic resources of finger&amp;nbsp;millet will enhance the novel breeding possibilities to address potential challenges of finger millet improvement."/>
        <meta property="og:image" content="https://media.springernature.com/w200/springer-static/cover/journal/12864.jpg"/>

                        <h1 class="c-article-title" data-test="article-title" data-article-title="" itemprop="name headline">Genome and Transcriptome sequence of Finger millet (<i>Eleusine coracana</i> (L.) Gaertn.) provides insights into drought tolerance and nutraceutical properties</h1>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Background</h3><p>Finger millet (<i>Eleusine coracana</i> (L.) Gaertn.) is an important staple food crop widely grown in Africa and South Asia. Among the millets, finger millet has high amount of calcium, methionine, tryptophan, fiber, and sulphur containing amino acids. In addition, it has C4 photosynthetic carbon assimilation mechanism, which helps to utilize water and nitrogen efficiently under hot and arid conditions without severely affecting yield. Therefore, development and utilization of genomic resources for genetic improvement of this crop is immensely useful.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Results</h3><p>Experimental results from whole genome sequencing and assembling process of ML-365 finger millet cultivar yielded 1196 Mb covering approximately 82% of total estimated genome size. Genome analysis showed the presence of 85,243 genes and one half of the genome is repetitive in nature. The finger millet genome was found to have higher colinearity with foxtail millet and rice as compared to other <i>Poaceae</i> species. Mining of simple sequence repeats (SSRs) yielded abundance of SSRs within the finger millet genome. Functional annotation and mining of transcription factors revealed finger millet genome harbors large number of drought tolerance related genes. Transcriptome analysis of low moisture stress and non-stress samples revealed the identification of several drought-induced candidate genes, which could be used in drought tolerance breeding.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Conclusions</h3><p>This genome sequencing effort will strengthen plant breeders for allele discovery, genetic mapping, and identification of candidate genes for agronomically important traits. Availability of genomic resources of finger millet will enhance the novel breeding possibilities to address potential challenges of finger millet improvement.</p></div></div></section>
                    
    


                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Background"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Background</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Finger millet (<i>Eleusine coracana</i> (L.) Gaertn.) is an allotetraploid (2n = 4× = 36, AABB) annual cereal millet crop that includes two distinct subspecies: subsp. <i>coracana</i> (cultivated finger millet or ragi) and subsp. <i>africana</i> (wild finger millet) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Hilu K. Validation of the combination Eleusine coracana subspecies africana (Kennedy-O’Byrne) Hilu et Dewet. Phytologia. 1994;76(5):410–1." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d52973e742">1</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Hilu KW, De Wet J. Domestication of Eleusine coracana. Econ Bot. 1976;30(3):199–208." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d52973e745">2</a>]. Based on morphological, cytogenetic, and molecular evidences, it is believed that modern finger millet (<i>E. coracana</i> subsp. <i>coracana</i>) is domesticated from wild finger millet (<i>E. coracaca</i> subsp. <i>africana</i>) populations [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Hilu KW, De Wet J. Domestication of Eleusine coracana. Econ Bot. 1976;30(3):199–208." href="#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d52973e761">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Chennaveeraiah M, Hiremath S. Genome analysis of Eleusine coracana (L.) Gaertn. Euphytica. 1974;23(3):489–95." href="#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d52973e761_1">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Dida MM, Wanyera N, Dunn MLH, Bennetzen JL, Devos KM. Population structure and diversity in finger millet (Eleusine coracana) germplasm. Trop Plant Biol. 2008;1(2):131–41." href="#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d52973e761_2">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Hilu K, Johnson J. Ribosomal DNA variation in finger millet and wild species of Eleusine (Poaceae). Theor Appl Genet. 1992;83(6–7):895–902." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d52973e764">5</a>]. The <i>E. coracana</i> subsp. <i>coracana</i> was domesticated around 5000 years ago in western Uganda and the Ethiopian highlands. Subsequently, finger millet was introduced into Western Ghats of India around 3000 BC. Thus India became the secondary centre of diversity for finger millet. It is the fourth most important millet after sorghum, pearl millet and foxtail millet [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Upadhyaya H, Gowda C, Reddy VG. Morphological diversity in finger millet germplasm introduced from Southern and Eastern Africa. J SAT Agric Res. 2007;3(1):1–3." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d52973e774">6</a>]. It occupies 12% of global millet’s area and is cultivated in more than 25 countries in African and Asian continents [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Vetriventhan M, Upadhyaya HD, Dwivedi SL, Pattanashetti SK, Singh SK. Finger and foxtail millets. In: Genetic and Genomic Resources for Grain Cereals Improvement. USA: Academic Press, Elsevier; 2015. p. 291–319." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d52973e777">7</a>]. Finger millet is potentially a climate-resilient and nutritious crop with high nutraceutical and antioxidant properties [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Kumar A, Metwal M, Kaur S, Gupta AK, Puranik S, Singh S, Singh M, Gupta S, Babu BK, Sood S. Nutraceutical Value of Finger Millet [Eleusine coracana (L.) Gaertn.], and Their Improvement Using Omics Approaches. Front Plant Sci. 2016;7. doi:&#xA;                    10.3389/fpls.2016.00934&#xA;                    &#xA;                  ." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d52973e780">8</a>]. Very importantly, finger millet grain is gluten-free, rich in calcium, fiber and iron, has excellent malting qualities, with low glycemic index (GI) and because of these properties, finger millet is a choice food for diabetics [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Chandrashekar A. Finger millet: Eleusine coracana. Adv Food Nutr Res. 2010;59:215–62." href="#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d52973e783">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Duke JA, Wain K. Medicinal plants of the world. In: Computer index with more than 85000 entries. Beltsville, Maryland: Plant Genetics and Germplasm Institute, Agricultural Research Service; 1981: 12–16." href="#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d52973e783_1">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Pradhan A, Nag S, Patil S. Dietary management of finger millet (Eleusine coracana L. Gaerth) controls diabetes. Curr Sci. 2010;98(6):763–5." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d52973e787">11</a>]. In sub-Saharan Africa and South Asia, millets are the survival food crops for resource-poor people. It produces reasonable grain and fodder yields under low input crop production systems and even survives on soils of low fertility. Finger millet grain is used to prepare diverse food cuisines and is highly nutritious. Apart from having rich nutritional value, it has an efficient carbon concentrating mechanism through C4 pathway. Genes involved in C4 mechanism could be one of the reasons for its hardy nature under low moisture and hot environmental conditions. It is worth comparing genes involved in C4 carbon assimilation network with already characterized genes from other cereal crops [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wang X, Gowik U, Tang H, Bowers JE, Westhoff P, Paterson AH. Comparative genomic analysis of C4 photosynthetic pathway evolution in grasses. Genome Biol. 2009;10(6):1." href="#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d52973e790">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Huang P, Studer AJ, Schnable JC, Kellogg EA, Brutnell TP: Cross species selection scans identify components of C4 photosynthesis in the grasses. J Exp Bot. 2016;68(2):127–135. &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1093/jxb/erw256&#xA;                    &#xA;                  ." href="#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d52973e790_1">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Saha D, Gowda MC, Arya L, Verma M, Bansal KC. Genetic and genomic resources of small millets. Crit Rev Plant Sci. 2016;35(1):56–79." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d52973e793">14</a>] and understand the variation at genomic level. In spite of its immense importance in human diet, development of genomic resources and high throughput breeding efforts have been limited. Considering these, we developed the whole genome sequence data for the short duration (110–115 days), high yielding (5.0–5.5 tonnes per hectare), drought tolerant finger millet variety ML-365, which was bred and released in 2008 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Gandhi RV, Hittalmani S, Babu NN. Performance of new ragi variety ML 365 by adopting production technology in southern Karnataka, India. Int J Plant Sci. 2012;7(1):176–80." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d52973e796">15</a>]. The present study describes the first <i>de novo</i> genome assembly of orphan nutri-cereal finger millet using combination of Illumina and SOLiD sequencing technologies. The whole genome assembly, SSRs, C4 pathway genes, resistance genes, drought responsive genes and calcium transport and regulation genes will be invaluable genomic resources for the future finger millet studies.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome size estimation of finger millet and wild species</h3><p>The ML-365 finger millet variety was developed through recombination breeding by crossing IE1012 and Indaf-5 in our laboratory earlier. Seeds of ML-365 (<i>Eleusine coracana</i> (L.) Gaertn. subsp. <i>coracana</i>), EC516253 (<i>Eleusine jaegeri</i> Pilg.), EC516251 (<i>Eleusine multiflora</i> Hochst. Ex A. Rich.), EC516248 (<i>Eleusine tristachya</i> Lam.), EC516245, EC516243 (<i>Eleusine indica</i> (L.) Gaertn.), EC541533, and EC541538 (<i>Eleusine coracana subsp. africana</i>) were grown in pots containing red soil and fertilizer mix. Young leaves were collected and chopped into pieces using sterilized blade and stained using nuclear isolation buffer (NIB). The NIB composed of hypotonic Propidium Iodide (PI), 50 μg/ml in 3 g/L trisodium citrate dihydride containing 0.05% (<i>v</i>/v) of Nonidet P-40 containing 2 mg/mL RNaseA stored in a dark amber bottle in a refrigerator. The liquid was filtered through 30 μm nylon mesh and samples were processed for ploidy estimation as per the protocol suggested by Krishan [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Krishan A. Rapid flow cytofluorometric analysis of mammalian cell cycle by propidium iodide staining. J Cell Biol. 1975;66(1):188–93." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d52973e839">16</a>]. Stained nuclei were analyzed using BD FACS cell sorter at Central Imaging and Flow Cytometry Facility (CIFF), Centre for Cellular and Molecular Platforms (C-CAMP), NCBS, Bengaluru, India. Genome sizes of all <i>Eleusine</i> species were estimated by comparing with <i>Pisum sativum</i> as an internal standard since, its nuclear genome is more stable [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Baranyi M, Greilhuber J. Flow cytometric analysis of genome size variation in cultivated and Wildpisum sativum (Fabaceae). Plant Syst Evol. 1995;194(3):231–9." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d52973e849">17</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Baranyi M, Greilhuber J, Swięcicki W. Genome size in wild Pisum species. TAG Theor Appl Genet. 1996;93(5):717–21." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d52973e852">18</a>] and ease in preparation of high quality nuclei suspension, which appear to be free of compounds interfere with PI staining [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Doležel J, Bartoš J. Plant DNA flow cytometry and estimation of nuclear genome size. Ann Bot. 2005;95(1):99–110." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d52973e855">19</a>]. The genome size was derived by multiplying the 1C value (pg) with 965 Mb (1 pg equivalent value) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Bennett MD, Smith J: Nuclear DNA amounts in angiosperms. Philos Trans R Soc Lond Ser B Biol Sci 1976, 274(933):227–274." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d52973e858">20</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Nucleic acid isolation</h3><p>Genomic DNA (gDNA) was isolated from all cultivated and wild species as per the manufacturer’s instruction (Cat # 69104, DNAeasy Plant Mini Kit) and DNA quality was checked by Nanodrop. The ML-365 finger millet variety was grown in well-watered (WW) and low moisture stress (LMS) conditions in pots under green house condition. Moisture stress was induced on the 80<sup>th</sup> day after sowing for 10 days [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Ramegowda V, Senthil-Kumar M, Nataraja KN, Reddy MK, Mysore KS, Udayakumar M. Expression of a finger millet transcription factor, EcNAC1, in tobacco confers abiotic stress-tolerance. PLoS One. 2012;7(7):e40397." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d52973e871">21</a>]. Leaf tissue was sampled on 91<sup>st</sup> day from WW (soil moisture level was 18.43%) and LMS (soil moisture level was 9.65%) plants. Total RNA was isolated using TRIzol reagent (Catalog #15596026, Invitrogen) and genomic DNA contamination was removed by DNase (Catalogue # AM1906, Ambion) treatment. The RNA Integrity and quantity was checked on Bioanalyzer using Agilent RNA 6000 nano chip.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Paired-end, mate pair library preparation and sequencing</h3><p>Genomic DNA was used to prepare two independent Illumina paired-end (PE) libraries (2 × 151, and 2 × 150 nts) by following manufacturer’s instruction using NEXTFlex DNA sequencing kit (catalog # 5140–02, Bioo Scientific). Pired-end libraries were sequenced using Illumina HiSeq4000 and NextSeq500. Also, three Illumina mate-pair (MP) libraries of insert size 2.5 Kb were prepared using Illumina Nextera Mate Pair sample preparation gel plus protocol (catalog # FC-132-1001, Illumina). To prepare SOLiD mate pair library, 3 μg of gDNA was fragmented on Covaris S220 and fragmented sample was size selected on 1% Agarose gel at the range of 3–4 Kb. The size distribution was checked on Agilent Bioanalyzer high sensitivity chip. Then SOLiD MP library was prepared as per the SOLiD mate pair library prep protocol and library was sequenced (2 × 61 nts) on SOLiD 5500 platform. All the sequencing work was carried out at Genotypic Technology Private Limited, Bengaluru, India.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">DNA-seq data pre-processing and <i>de novo</i> genome assembly</h3><p>The raw reads of PE and MP libraries were trimmed for adapter sequence contamination and low-quality bases (PHRED score of &lt;Q30) using FASTX-ToolKit (<a href="http://hannonlab.cshl.edu/fastx_toolkit">http://hannonlab.cshl.edu/fastx_toolkit/</a>). The SOAPdenovo2 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Luo R, Liu B, Xie Y, Li Z, Huang W, Yuan J, et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience. 2012;1(1):18." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d52973e903">22</a>] assembler was used to assemble all the high-quality PE and MP reads. The contigs were further scaffolded using SSPACE [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Boetzer M, Henkel CV, Jansen HJ, Butler D, Pirovano W. Scaffolding pre-assembled contigs using SSPACE. Bioinformatics. 2011;27(4):578–9." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d52973e906">23</a>] and gaps in the scaffolds were closed by GapCloser module in the SOAPdenovo2 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Luo R, Liu B, Xie Y, Li Z, Huang W, Yuan J, et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience. 2012;1(1):18." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d52973e909">22</a>]. The genome completeness of assembly was checked by CEGMA [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Parra G, Bradnam K, Korf I. CEGMA: a pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genomes. Bioinformatics. 2007;23(9):1061–7." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d52973e912">24</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Gene prediction and functional annotation</h3><p>The assembled scaffolds were used to predict the genes using AUGUSTUS [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Stanke M, Keller O, Gunduz I, Hayes A, Waack S, Morgenstern B. AUGUSTUS: ab initio prediction of alternative transcripts. Nucleic Acids Res. 2006;34(suppl 2):W435–9." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d52973e924">25</a>] by <i>Zea mays</i> as a reference gene model. In addition, RNA-seq data was incorporated to AUGUSTUS to assist the gene prediction. The gene ontology annotation was carried out by performing a BLAST with the protein sequences of Viridiplantae retrieved from UniProt database. The pathway analysis of genes was carried out by KAAS server [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Moriya Y, Itoh M, Okuda S, Yoshizawa AC, Kanehisa M. KAAS: an automatic genome annotation and pathway reconstruction server. Nucleic Acids Res. 2007;35(suppl 2):W182–5." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d52973e930">26</a>] using <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> (ath), <i>Glycine max</i> (gmx), <i>Oryza sativa japonica</i> (osa), and <i>Vitis vinifera</i> (vvi) as reference organisms. The protein domain structures of all protein coding genes were identified using InterProScan5 software [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Jones P, Binns D, Chang H-Y, Fraser M, Li W, McAnulla C, et al. InterProScan 5: genome-scale protein function classification. Bioinformatics. 2014;30(9):1236–40." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d52973e946">27</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Gene families, phylogenetic analysis and mining of transcription factors</h3><p>The protein sequences of maize (<a href="ftp://ftp.ensemblgenomes.org/pub/plants/release-22/fasta/zea_mays/pep">ftp://ftp.ensemblgenomes.org/pub/plants/release-22/fasta/zea_mays/pep/</a>), sorghum (<a href="ftp://ftp.ensemblgenomes.org/pub/plants/release-31/fasta/sorghum_bicolor/pep">ftp://ftp.ensemblgenomes.org/pub/plants/release-31/fasta/sorghum_bicolor/pep/</a>), rice (<a href="ftp://ftp.plantbiology.msu.edu/pub/data/Eukaryotic_Projects/o_sativa/annotation_dbs/pseudomolecules/version_7.0/all.dir">ftp://ftp.plantbiology.msu.edu/pub/data/Eukaryotic_Projects/o_sativa/annotation_dbs/pseudomolecules/version_7.0/all.dir/</a>), foxtail millet (<a href="http://foxtailmillet.genomics.org.cn/page/species/download.jsp">http://foxtailmillet.genomics.org.cn/page/species/download.jsp</a>), and <i>Brachypodium (</i>
                           <a href="ftp://ftp.ensemblgenomes.org/pub/plants/release-31/fasta/brachypodium_distachyon/pep">ftp://ftp.ensemblgenomes.org/pub/plants/release-31/fasta/brachypodium_distachyon/pep/</a>) were downloaded. The paralogs and orthologs among finger millet, rice, sorghum, maize, foxtail millet, and <i>Brachypodium</i> were identified using OrthoMCL [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Li L, Stoeckert CJ, Roos DS. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 2003;13(9):2178–89." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d52973e999">28</a>]. The groups having at least one copy of gene from each genome were considered as core orthologous groups (COGs). Based on orthoMCL clustering, protein sequences of single copy ortholog gene groups from six species were aligned and inferred phylogenetic relationship as described previously [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Hittalmani S, Mahesh H, Mahadevaiah C, Prasannakumar MK. De novo genome assembly and annotation of rice sheath rot fungus Sarocladium oryzae reveals genes involved in Helvolic acid and Cerulenin biosynthesis pathways. BMC Genomics. 2016;17(1):1." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d52973e1002">29</a>]. The consensus phylogenetic tree was drawn using FigTree V1.4.2 (<a href="http://tree.bio.ed.ac.uk/software/figtree">http://tree.bio.ed.ac.uk/software/figtree/</a>). Scaffolds of finger millet genome was concatenated and syntenic relationship among <i>Poaceae</i> species was inferred by Symap tool [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Soderlund C, Nelson W, Shoemaker A, Paterson A. SyMAP: A system for discovering and viewing syntenic regions of FPC maps. Genome Res. 2006;16(9):1159–68." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d52973e1016">30</a>]. Protein sequences of plant transcription factors (TFs) were retrieved from plant transcription factor database (<a href="http://planttfdb.cbi.pku.edu.cn/index.php">http://planttfdb.cbi.pku.edu.cn/index.php</a>) and used as a reference to identify TFs in ML-365 genes by BLASTP (default e-value cutoff = 10<sup>−10</sup>, minimum identity = 40% and minimum query coverage = 50%) alignments.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Analysis of calcium transporter, disease resistant and C4 pathway genes</h3><p>We retrieved gene sequences from NCBI database involved in calcium transporter process based on functional annotation like Ca(2+)/H(+) antiporter (CAX1), two pore channel (TPC1), CaM-stimulated type IIB Ca(2+) ATPase, Calmodulin dependent protein kinase 1 and 2 (CaMK) based on previous studies [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Mirza N, Taj G, Arora S, Kumar A. Transcriptional expression analysis of genes involved in regulation of calcium translocation and storage in finger millet (Eleusine coracana L. Gartn.). Gene. 2014;550(2):171–9." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d52973e1036">31</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Singh UM, Metwal M, Singh M, Taj G, Kumar A. Identification and characterization of calcium transporter gene family in finger millet in relation to grain calcium content. Gene. 2015;566(1):37–46." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d52973e1039">32</a>]. The predicted Finger millet protein sequences were queried against these protein sequences using BLASTP analysis. Also, the genes containing leucine rich repeats (LRR), coiled-coil (CC), protein kinases (PKs) and NB-ARC domains were identified in finger millet predicted gene set based on protein family (Pfam) annotation using InterProScan5 software [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Jones P, Binns D, Chang H-Y, Fraser M, Li W, McAnulla C, et al. InterProScan 5: genome-scale protein function classification. Bioinformatics. 2014;30(9):1236–40." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d52973e1042">27</a>]. In order to understand the genes involved in C4 mechanism, previously characterized C4 pathway genes from different cereals were used as a reference [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Wang X, Gowik U, Tang H, Bowers JE, Westhoff P, Paterson AH. Comparative genomic analysis of C4 photosynthetic pathway evolution in grasses. Genome Biol. 2009;10(6):1." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d52973e1045">12</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Huang P, Studer AJ, Schnable JC, Kellogg EA, Brutnell TP: Cross species selection scans identify components of C4 photosynthesis in the grasses. J Exp Bot. 2016;68(2):127–135. &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1093/jxb/erw256&#xA;                    &#xA;                  ." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d52973e1048">13</a>] to identify C4 genes in finger millet genome. Homologs with highest identity were selected from finger millet sequenced data for each of the seven C4 pathway genes viz., phosphoenolpyruvate carboxylase (PEPC), PEPC kinase (PPCK), NADP-malate dehydrogenase (NADP-MDH), NADP-malic enzyme (NADP-ME), pyruvate orthophosphate dikinase (PPDK), PPDK-regulatory protein (PPDK-RP), and carbonic anhydrase (CA). Ortholog genes from finger millet genome were used for multiple sequence alignment (MSA) and phylogenetic analyses with other C4 cereal genes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Li W, Cowley A, Uludag M, Gur T, McWilliam H, Squizzato S, et al. The EMBL-EBI bioinformatics web and programmatic tools framework. Nucleic Acids Res. 2015;43(W1):W580–4." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d52973e1052">33</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">RNA-seq library preparation and data pre-processing</h3><p>Around 200 ng of total RNA from well-watered (WW) and low moisture stress (LMS) samples was taken and independent paired-end (PE) libraries were prepared using SureSelect Strand Specific RNA library Prep ILM kit (Cat# 5500–0135, Agilent Technologies). The final libraries were quantified using Qubit and quality was validated on Agilent D1000 ScreenTape system. Finally, the adapter containing fragments were quantified using KAPA library quantification kit (Catalog # KK4824, KAPA Biosystems) and RNA-seq libraries were sequenced (2 × 150 nts) using Illumina NextSeq500. The raw reads were pre-processed for adapter contamination and low-quality bases (PHRED score &lt; 30) using FASTX-ToolKit (<a href="http://hannonlab.cshl.edu/fastx_toolkit">http://hannonlab.cshl.edu/fastx_toolkit/</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Transcriptome assembly, differential gene analysis and functional annotation</h3><p>High quality pre-processed reads from WW and LMS samples were assembled independently using Trinity [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Grabherr MG, Haas BJ, Yassour M, Levin JZ, Thompson DA, Amit I, et al. Trinity: reconstructing a full-length transcriptome without a genome from RNA-Seq data. Nat Biotechnol. 2011;29(7):644–52." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d52973e1078">34</a>]. Unigenes were identified by clustering (90% homology and 95% query coverage) the putative transcripts (PUTs) of WW and LMS samples using CD-Hit [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Li W, Godzik A. Cd-hit: a fast program for clustering and comparing large sets of protein or nucleotide sequences. Bioinformatics. 2006;22(13):1658–9." href="#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d52973e1081">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Annadurai RS, Neethiraj R, Jayakumar V, Damodaran AC, Rao SN, Katta MA, et al. De Novo transcriptome assembly (NGS) of Curcuma longa L. rhizome reveals novel transcripts related to anticancer and antimalarial terpenoids. PLoS One. 2013;8(2):e56217." href="#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d52973e1081_1">36</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Gahlan P, Singh HR, Shankar R, Sharma N, Kumari A, Chawla V, et al. De novo sequencing and characterization of Picrorhiza kurrooa transcriptome at two temperatures showed major transcriptome adjustments. BMC Genomics. 2012;13(1):126." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d52973e1084">37</a>]. RNA-seq reads were mapped to unigenes set using Bowtie2 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Langmead B, Salzberg SL. Fast gapped-read alignment with Bowtie 2. Nat Methods. 2012;9(4):357–9." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d52973e1087">38</a>] and number of reads mapped per unigenes was counted by in-house script. Differential gene expression between WW and LMS samples was analyzed using DESeq [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Anders S, Huber W. Differential expression analysis for sequence count data. Genome Biol. 2010;11(10):1." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d52973e1090">39</a>]. Functional annotation of putative transcripts was performed by comparing Viridiplantae protein sequences in UniProt database (<a href="http://www.uniprot.org/taxonomy/33090">http://www.uniprot.org/taxonomy/33090</a>). Transcripts with more than 30% protein homology were considered for downstream analysis.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Validation of drought responsive genes through qRT-PCR</h3><p>Equal amount of total RNA of WW and LMS samples was used to synthesize complementary DNA (cDNA) using RevertAid First Strand cDNA synthesis kit (K1622, ThermoFisher Scientific). Twenty micro litres of reaction mixture containing SYBR Premix Ex Taq (RR420A, TaKaRa), ROX reference dye, 10 μM of each of forward and reverse primers, and template cDNA was used to perform qRT-PCR in Corbett RotorGene 6000. Two biological replicates and three technical replicates were amplified for all samples. All the ‘Ct’ values were normalized based on ‘Ct’ value of elongation factor housekeeping gene and differential gene expression (fold change) was calculated as per 2<sup>-ΔΔCt</sup> method.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Simple and complex repeat prediction</h3><p>Transposable elements (TEs) were identified in finger millet genome using a combination of <i>de novo</i> and reference based approaches. Finger millet repeat database was built using RepeatModeler and repeats were predicted using RepeatMasker 4.0.5 (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org">http://www.repeatmasker.org</a>). Similar approach was followed to identify TEs in Trinity assembled transcripts from RNA-seq data. The simple sequence repeats (SSRs) were predicted genome-wide using Microsatellite Identification tool [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Thiel T, Michalek W, Varshney R, Graner A. Exploiting EST databases for the development and characterization of gene-derived SSR-markers in barley (Hordeum vulgare L.). Theor Appl Genet. 2003;106(3):411–22." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d52973e1130">40</a>] (<a href="http://pgrc.ipk-gatersleben.de/misa/misa.html">http://pgrc.ipk-gatersleben.de/misa/misa.html</a>) and categorized as Class I and II based on the previous report [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Temnykh S, DeClerck G, Lukashova A, Lipovich L, Cartinhour S, McCouch S. Computational and experimental analysis of microsatellites in rice (Oryza sativa L.): frequency, length variation, transposon associations, and genetic marker potential. Genome Res. 2001;11(8):1441–52." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d52973e1140">41</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Primer designing for SSRs and electronic PCR</h3><p>Primers were designed for flanking sequences of SSRs using Primer3 tool (the criteria set were like GC = 50–55%, Tm = 55–65 °C and product length = 100–350 bp). Primer sequences overlapping with repetitive elements were eliminated. The efficacy of designed primers was checked by e-PCR [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Schuler GD. Sequence mapping by electronic PCR. Genome Res. 1997;7(5):541–50." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d52973e1151">42</a>] (five bases mismatches were allowed at a primer binding site) and primer sequences hitting multiple sites in the genome were eliminated. Randomly, 35 SSRs comprising di-, tri-, tetra-, penta-, and hexa-nucleotide types were selected for wet lab validation.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Oligo synthesis and genotyping of finger millet accessions</h3><p>Oligos were synthesized at Eurofins Genomics Private Limited, Bengaluru, India. DNA from young leaf samples was isolated using CTAB method [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Dellaporta S, Wood J, Hicks J. A plant DNA minipreparation: Version II. Plant Mol Biol Rep. 1983;1(4):19–21." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d52973e1162">43</a>]. We set up 10 μl volume PCR reaction containing 20 ng of genomic DNA, 5 μl of emeraldAMP GT PCR master mix (Cat # RR310A, Takara), 0.5 μl of 10 mM of each forward and reverse primers. PCR amplification was carried out in Eppendorf vapo protect with initial denaturation temperature at 95<sup>o</sup> C for 3 min followed by 30 cycles with 30 s at 95<sup>o</sup> C, 30 s of annealing temperature (55<sup>o</sup> C), 1 min of 72<sup>o</sup> C, final extension for 5 min at 72<sup>o</sup> C. PCR products were resolved on 3.5% agarose and 8% PAGE (only for monomorphic primers) stained with Ethidium bromide (final concentration at 0.5 μg/mL) and visualized using gel documentation unit.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec16" data-title="Results and Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec16-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec16">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec16-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Estimation of genome size of ML-365 finger millet variety and related eleusine species</h3><p>ML-365 variety was developed in our laboratory as a part of collaborative research funded by the McKnight Foundation, USA (<a href="http://www.ccrp.org/sites/default/files/finger_millet___year_4___development_of_high-yielding_disease-resistant_and_drought-tolerant_finger_millet_genotypes.pdf">http://www.ccrp.org/sites/default/files/finger_millet___year_4___development_of_high-yielding_disease-resistant_and_drought-tolerant_finger_millet_genotypes.pdf</a>) through farmer’s participatory varietal selection approach and released for commercial cultivation in 2008 in India. It is a drought tolerant, blast disease resistant variety possessing good cooking qualities preferred by farmers and consumers. Over the years, it has spread across several thousands of hectares and survives very well under long spells of drought.</p><p>In order to estimate the genome size of cultivated and wild species of <i>Eleusine,</i> the samples were subjected to flow cytometry analysis. One accession each of <i>E. jaegeri</i>, <i>E. multiflora,</i> and ML-365, and two accessions of <i>E. triastachya</i>, <i>E. indica</i>, <i>E. coracana</i> subsp. <i>africana</i> species were chosen. The average DNA content (2C value) of <i>E. jaegeri</i>, <i>E. multiflora</i>, <i>E. tristachya</i>, <i>E. indica</i>, <i>E. coracana</i> subsp. <i>africana</i> was 1.20 pg, 1.84 pg, 1.14 pg, 1.21 pg, 2.52 pg, respectively. The genome size of wild species ranged from 580 Mb (<i>E. jaegeri</i>) to 1217 Mb (<i>E. coracana</i> subsp. <i>africana</i>). The total DNA content (2C value) and genome size of ML-365 (<i>E. coracana</i>) were 3.01 pg and 1453 Mb, respectively (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#Fig1">1</a>). The genome sizes of <i>E. coracana</i> subsp. <i>coracana</i> and <i>E. coracana</i> subsp<i>. africana</i> are relatively similar. This could be because, finger millet was domesticated from <i>E. coracana</i> subsp<i>. africana</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Hilu KW, De Wet J. Domestication of Eleusine coracana. Econ Bot. 1976;30(3):199–208." href="#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d52973e1275">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Chennaveeraiah M, Hiremath S. Genome analysis of Eleusine coracana (L.) Gaertn. Euphytica. 1974;23(3):489–95." href="#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d52973e1275_1">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Dida MM, Wanyera N, Dunn MLH, Bennetzen JL, Devos KM. Population structure and diversity in finger millet (Eleusine coracana) germplasm. Trop Plant Biol. 2008;1(2):131–41." href="#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d52973e1275_2">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Hilu K, Johnson J. Ribosomal DNA variation in finger millet and wild species of Eleusine (Poaceae). Theor Appl Genet. 1992;83(6–7):895–902." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d52973e1278">5</a>]. Several studies indicated that <i>E. indica</i> could be the maternal donor of ‘A’ genome of <i>E. coracana</i> subsp. <i>coracana</i> and ‘B’ genome donor is yet to be deciphered [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Neves SS, Swire-Clark G, Hilu KW, Baird WV. Phylogeny of Eleusine (Poaceae: Chloridoideae) based on nuclear ITS and plastid trnT–trnF sequences. Mol Phylogen Evol. 2005;35(2):395–419." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d52973e1291">44</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Hilu KW. Identification of the “A” genome of finger millet using chloroplast DNA. Genetics. 1988;118(1):163–7." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d52973e1294">45</a>]. Few studies showed that <i>E. floccifolia</i> is the ‘B’ genome donor [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Bisht M, Mukai Y. Genomic in situ hybridization identifies genome donor of finger millet (Eleusine coracana). Theor Appl Genet. 2001;102(6–7):825–32." href="#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d52973e1300">46</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Bisht MS, Mukai Y. Mapping of rDNA on the chromosomes of Eleusine species by fluorescence in situ hybridization. Genes Genet Syst. 2000;75(6):343–8." href="#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d52973e1300_1">47</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Bisht MS, Mukai Y. Identification of Genome Donors to the Wild Species of Finger Millet, Eleusine africana by Genomic in situ Hybridization. Breed Sci. 2001;51(4):263–9." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d52973e1303">48</a>]. However, waxy sequence and multicolor genomic in situ hybridization (McGISH) studies revealed that ‘B’ genome donor must have become extinct or yet to be uncovered [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Liu Q, Jiang B, Wen J, Peterson PM. Low-copy nuclear gene and McGISH resolves polyploid history of Eleusine coracana and morphological character evolution in Eleusine. Turk J Bot. 2014;38(1):1–12." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d52973e1306">49</a>]. Genome sequencing of wild species of ‘A’ and ‘B’ genomes, comparison with tetraploid cultivated species (<i>E. coracana</i> subsp. <i>coracana</i>) resolve the ambiguity of the progenitors.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Fig. 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-017-3850-z/MediaObjects/12864_2017_3850_Fig1_HTML.gif?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-017-3850-z/MediaObjects/12864_2017_3850_Fig1_HTML.gif" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>The species of <i>Eleusine</i> and their chromosome number, genome designation, ploidy level and flow cytometry analysis. In case of flow cytometry results ‘x’ and ‘y’ axes represents nuclei counts and relative fluorescence intensity, respectively</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Genome sequencing and genome assembly</h3><p>Around 45 Gb of paired-end and 21 Gb of mate-pair data was generated using Illumina and SOLiD sequencing technologies (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#MOESM1">1</a>). Combination of paired-end and mate pair reads were used to assemble the ML-365 genome followed by gap closing yielded 1196 Mb of consensus genome size, representing 82.31% of the estimated finger millet genome (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#MOESM2">2</a>). The assembly consisted of 525,759 scaffolds (&gt;200 bp) with N50 length of 23.73 Kb (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#Tab1">1</a>) and the average length of scaffolds was 2275 bp. Genome assembly was filtered for mitochondrion, chloroplast, fungal, and bacterial sequence contaminations. We further evaluated the ML-365 scaffolds for genome completeness by Core Eukaryotic Gene Mapping Approach (CEGMA) tool. Results showed that around 94.35% of core eukaryotic genes (CEG) were present in the ML-365 genome (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#MOESM3">3</a>). Highly self-pollinated behavior, small flower size, narrow genetic base of germplasm, lack of precise information on trait values of wild species for their inclusion in finger millet improvement, highly neglected in terms of research as compared to crops like rice, wheat and maize at national and international level, are the major factors behind limited breeding improvements of this crop [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Sood S, Babu BK. Finger Millet. In: Broadening the Genetic Base of Grain Cereals. India: Springer. 2016:225–256." href="#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d52973e1358">50</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Oduori C, Kanyenji B. Finger millet in Kenya: Importance, advances in R&amp;D, challenges and opportunities for improved production and profitability. Finger Millet Blast Management in East Africa Creating opportunities for improving production and utilization of finger millet. Nairobi: Proceedings of the First International Finger Millet Stakeholder Workshop. 2005:10–22." href="#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d52973e1358_1">51</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Upadhyaya HD, Gowda CL, Pundir RP, Reddy VG, Singh S. Development of core subset of finger millet germplasm using geographical origin and data on 14 quantitative traits. Genet Resour Crop Evol. 2006;53(4):679–85." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d52973e1362">52</a>]. However, hybridization between Indian and African germplasm resulted into development of several ‘Indaf’ series of finger millet varieties, which have gained popularity in the farmer’s field. Later on, the first major milestone in finger millet genomics is the development of first detailed genetic linkage map in F<sub>2</sub> population by crossing <i>E. coracana</i> subsp. <i>coracana</i> cv. Okhale-1 and its wild progenitor <i>E. coracana</i> subsp. <i>africana</i> acc. MD-20 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Dida MM, Ramakrishnan S, Bennetzen JL, Gale MD, Devos KM. The genetic map of finger millet, Eleusine coracana. Theor Appl Genet. 2007;114(2):321–32." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d52973e1380">53</a>]. Since then, few researchers have concentrated on assessing the genetic variability among finger millet population and QTL mapping for important traits [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Dida MM, Wanyera N, Dunn MLH, Bennetzen JL, Devos KM. Population structure and diversity in finger millet (Eleusine coracana) germplasm. Trop Plant Biol. 2008;1(2):131–41." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d52973e1383">4</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Yadav S, Gaur VS, Jaiswal J, Kumar A. Simple sequence repeat (SSR) analysis in relation to calcium transport and signaling genes reveals transferability among grasses and a conserved behavior within finger millet genotypes. Plant Syst Evol. 2014;300(6):1561–8." href="#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d52973e1386">54</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Kumar A, Yadav S, Panwar P, Gaur V, Sood S. Identification of Anchored Simple Sequence Repeat Markers Associated with Calcium Content in Finger Millet (Eleusine coracana). Proc Natl Acad Sci India B Biol Sci. 2015;85(1):311–7." href="#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d52973e1386_1">55</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Fakrudin B, Shashidhar H, Kulkarni R, Hittalmani S. Genetic diversity assessment of finger millet, Eleusine coracana (Gaertn), germplasm through RAPD analysis. PGR Newslett. 2004;138:50–4." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d52973e1389">56</a>]. However, gene cloning, Marker Assisted Selection (MAS), genome-wide association studies in finger millet are inadequate. The availability of whole genome assembly of ML-365 will expedite the improvement of finger millet.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Genome assembly statistics of ML-365</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Gene prediction and functional annotation of genes</h3><p>We predicted, 78,647 non-TE related and 6596 TE related genes summing up to a total of 85,243 genes in ML-365 genome based on <i>de novo</i> method of gene prediction using Augustus. Functional annotation confirmed that majority of the genes had homologs with known functions in UniProt protein database. The gene ontology (GO) annotation of genes revealed that more number of genes were involved in molecular function followed by cellular and biological processes. Majority of the genes were involved in nucleic acid binding, zinc ion binding, and ATP binding activities under molecular function. Similarly, DNA integration and integral components of membrane were major functions related to biological and cellular processes, respectively (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#Fig2">2a</a>). The KAAS server based pathway prediction showed that majority of genes were involved in carbohydrate metabolism, amino acid metabolism, translation, lipid metabolism and folding, sorting, and degradation pathways (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#Fig2">2b</a>). Out of 85,243, 52,541 genes contained Pfam domain as per InterProScan results and these genes were distributed across 3254 gene families. Among 3254, retrotransposon gag protein, gag-polypeptide of LTR copia-type, reverse transcriptase, zinc knuckle, putative gypsy type transposon, protein kinase, cytochrome P450, and integrase core domain were the major gene families.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Fig. 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-017-3850-z/MediaObjects/12864_2017_3850_Fig2_HTML.gif?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-017-3850-z/MediaObjects/12864_2017_3850_Fig2_HTML.gif" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>The gene ontology (<b>a</b>) and pathway prediction (<b>b</b>) of finger millet</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Gene family orthologs and synteny with other sequenced <i>Poaceae</i> species</h3><p>The ortholog gene families across species provide insight into co-evolution of plant species. Clustering of genes of six <i>Poaceae</i> species (Finger millet, Rice, Foxtail millet, Sorghum, Maize, and <i>Brachypodium</i>) using OrthoMCL tool resulted in 10,291 core orthologous groups (COGs) gene families shared by all six species (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#Fig3">3</a>). These families could have arisen due to whole genome duplication event. Among COGs gene families, 23,332 (35.22%) of rice, 24,427 (28.66%) of finger millet, 17,212 (47.37%) of sorghum, 27,401 (43.33%) of maize, 15,927 (41.05%) of foxtail millet and 17,888 (57.65%) of <i>Brachypodium</i> genes formed core gene families common to all species. However, within those COGs, 6107 (9.22%) of rice, 2976 (3.49%) of finger millet, 7337 (20.19%) of sorghum, 4403 (6.96%) of maize, 7879 (20.31%) of foxtail millet and 6999 (22.56%) of <i>Brachypodium</i> genes have retained single copy. Remaining genes were in multiple copies and these expansions or contractions of gene families have a significant role in diversification of flowering plants [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Purugganan MD, Rounsley SD, Schmidt RJ, Yanofsky MF. Molecular evolution of flower development: diversification of the plant MADS-box regulatory gene family. Genetics. 1995;140(1):345–56." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d52973e1639">57</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Bennetzen JL. Mechanisms and rates of genome expansion and contraction in flowering plants. Genetica. 2002;115(1):29–36." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d52973e1642">58</a>]. The expanded gene families belonged to zinc knuckle, reverse transcriptase, aspartyl protease, leucine rich repeat, gag-polypeptide, gypsy type transposon, integrase, and ATHILA ORF-1 family protein domain containing genes. Single copy orthologous gene (766 genes across six species) based phylogeny among six species revealed that finger millet is closer to rice followed by foxtail millet (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#Fig4">4</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Fig. 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-017-3850-z/MediaObjects/12864_2017_3850_Fig3_HTML.gif?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-017-3850-z/MediaObjects/12864_2017_3850_Fig3_HTML.gif" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Distribution of orthologous gene families among major <i>Poaceae</i> species. Numbers in parentheses indicate the number of genes used for clustering</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                           <div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Fig. 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-017-3850-z/MediaObjects/12864_2017_3850_Fig4_HTML.gif?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-017-3850-z/MediaObjects/12864_2017_3850_Fig4_HTML.gif" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Phylogenetic relationship of six <i>Poaceae</i> species revealed based on single copy ortholog genes</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <p>Intergenome collinear analyses between finger millet, <i>Brachypodium</i>, foxtail millet, sorghum, maize, and rice showed highly conserved genomic regions, signifying close evolutionary relationship among these grass species. In total, we identified 1592 large collinear blocks between finger millet and rice; 1709 between finger millet and foxtail millet; 1693 between finger millet and sorghum; 1613 between finger millet and maize; 436 between finger millet and <i>Brachypodium</i>, indicating 97%, 98%, 95%, 96%, and 82% of finger millet genome is colinear with these grass species, respectively (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#Fig5">5</a>). Previous comparative analysis also revealed more number of conserved genomic regions between finger millet and rice genomes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Srinivasachary, Dida MM, Gale MD, Devos KM. Comparative analyses reveal high levels of conserved colinearity between the finger millet and rice genomes. Theor Appl Genet. 2007;115(4):489–99." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d52973e1704">59</a>]. This syntenic relationship of finger millet with other cereal crops will enable us to map orthologous candidate Quantitative Trait Loci (QTLs) or genes of interest [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Babu BK, Agrawal P, Pandey D, Kumar A. Comparative genomics and association mapping approaches for opaque2 modifier genes in finger millet accessions using genic, genomic and candidate gene-based simple sequence repeat markers. Mol Breed. 2014;34(3):1261–79." href="#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d52973e1707">60</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Babu BK, Pandey D, Agrawal P, Sood S, Kumar A. In-silico mining, type and frequency analysis of genic microsatellites of finger millet (Eleusine coracana (L.) Gaertn.): a comparative genomic analysis of NBS–LRR regions of finger millet with rice. Mol Biol Rep. 2014;41(5):3081–90." href="#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d52973e1707_1">61</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Nath M, Goel A, Taj G, Kumar A. Molecular cloning and comparative in silico analysis of calmodulin genes from cereals and millets for understanding the mechanism of differential calcium accumulation. J Proteomics Bioinform. 2010;3:294–301." href="#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d52973e1707_2">62</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Wang L, Czedik-Eysenberg A, Mertz RA, Si Y, Tohge T, Nunes-Nesi A, et al. Comparative analyses of C4 and C3 photosynthesis in developing leaves of maize and rice. Nat Biotechnol. 2014;32(11):1158–65." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d52973e1711">63</a>].</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Fig. 5"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-017-3850-z/MediaObjects/12864_2017_3850_Fig5_HTML.gif?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-017-3850-z/MediaObjects/12864_2017_3850_Fig5_HTML.gif" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>Syntenic genomic blocks of Finger millet with other <i>Poaceae</i> species (Rice, Sorghum, Maize, Foxtail millet and <i>Brachypodium</i>)</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Repeat content in finger millet genome</h3><p>Transposable elements are the major components of eukaryotic genomes and their integration in the genome play a vital role in genome evolution and duplication. The assembled scaffolds were analysed for repeat content using RepeatModeller and RepeatMasker tools. We annotated finger millet genome using <i>de novo</i> constructed repeat library indicating around 49.92% of finger millet genome is repetitive. The retroelements constitutes around 35.56%, LTR elements were the major components (33.26%) among retroelements. Subsequently, unclassified repeats and DNA transposons were found in minor fractions of 9.73% and 4.48%, respectively (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#Tab2">2</a>). Previous report also indicated the richness of finger millet genome with repeats based on DNA reassociation kinetics [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Gupta VS, Ranjekar P. DNA sequence organization in finger millet (Eleusine coracana). J Biosci (Bangalore). 1981;3(4):417–30." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d52973e1754">64</a>]. This repetitive nature of the finger millet genome is attributed to larger lengths of interspersed DNA repeats as reported in pearl millet [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Gupta VS, Ranjekar P. DNA sequence organization in finger millet (Eleusine coracana). J Biosci (Bangalore). 1981;3(4):417–30." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d52973e1757">64</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Wimpee CF, Rawson JR. Characterization of the nuclear genome of pearl millet. Biochim Biophys Acta. 1979;562(2):192–206." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d52973e1760">65</a>]. We predicted the repeat content in a similar manner like genome in Trinity assembled putative transcripts, the overall repeat content was found to be 4.87%. Most of this fraction (4.28%) was constituted unclassified repeats and remaining was simple and low complexity repeats.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-2"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab2" data-test="table-caption">Table 2 Repeat content in ML-365 finger millet genome</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z/tables/2"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <p>Simple sequence repeats (SSRs) play an evolutionary relationship in genome evolution. The scaffolds of ML-365 genome were subjected for identification of SSRs using MISA. A total of 114,083 SSRs were distributed across ML-365 genome signifying the abundance of SSRs [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Gimode D, Odeny DA, de Villiers EP, Wanyonyi S, Dida MM, Mneney EE, et al. Identification of SNP and SSR Markers in Finger Millet Using Next Generation Sequencing Technologies. PLoS One. 2016;11(7):e0159437." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d52973e2064">66</a>], of which 66,805 (58.56%), 40,578 (35.57%), 2179 (1.91%), 3010 (2.64%) and 1511 (1.32%) were di-, tri-, tetra-, penta-, and hexa- types, respectively. Among di-repeats, AG/CT (66.64%) were highest followed by AT/AT (17.21%), AC/GT (12.96%) and CG/CG (3.20%) types. Similarly, AAG/CTT were the highest among tri-repeats types followed by CCG/CGG (15.13%), ATC/ATG (14%), AGG/CCT (13.19%), AAC/GTT (13.30%), ACC/GGT (7.55%), AAT/ATT (7.54%), AGC/CTG (7.47%), ACG/CGT (2.78%) and ACT/AGT (1.82%) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#Fig6">6</a>). In case of tetra type repeats, AAAT/ATTT (21.48%) and AAAG/CTTT (19.55%) were higher as compared to other types of tetra type repeats. The penta repeats, AAAAG/CTTTT (10.76%) and AAAAT/ATTTT (10.20%), and among the hexa repeats, AACACC/GGTGTT (13.70%) were found to be in higher proportion. Primers were designed for di-, tri-, tetra-, penta-, and hexa- types of SSRs using Primer3 software. Primer sequence flanking the repetitive regions and primers generating multiple amplimers were eliminated using ePCR and finally 18,514 SSRs were shortlisted for future applications (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#MOESM4">4</a>). The wet lab PCR validation of randomly chosen 35 SSRs in ML-365 confirmed the <i>in silico</i> e-PCR results (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#Fig6">6</a>). Also, fingerprinting of 26 finger millet accessions, 14 wild species revealed minor allelic variation on 3.5% agarose gel for 35 SSRs (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#MOESM5">5</a>). However, when PCR amplified products were resolved on PAGE, the extent of polymorphism observed was much higher. In addition, we observed higher polymorphism in case of wild species of finger millet as compared to <i>Eleusine coracana</i> germplasm on PAGE gels. The inherent drawback of low polymorphism in finger millet was observed in previous studies [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Salimath SS, Oliveira AC, Bennetzen JL, Godwin ID. Assessment of genome origins and genetic diversity in the genus Eleusine with DNA markers. Genome. 1995;38(4):757–63." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-017-3850-z#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d52973e2086">67</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Kumar A, Sharma D, Tiwari A, Jaiswal J, Singh N, Sood S. Genotyping-by-Sequencing analysis for determining population structure of finger millet germplasm of diverse origins.
